Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

Once upon a time, there was the sister of Scheherazade named Dunyazad, a child trying to go to Slumberland named Little Nemo and an English poet named Geoffery Chaucer. Little Nemo suggested that he should tell a story, because it was Alex's birthday. So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Little Nemo told:

There was once an engmatic labyrinth that was a map of itself. Virgil couldn't quite say how he was wandering there. Virgil walked away from that place. 

Virgil entered a cramped and narrow peristyle, dominated by moki steps framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. 

Virgil entered a luxurious fogou, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Virgil thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Virgil entered a rough tetrasoon, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Virgil felt sure that this must be the way out, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Virgil entered a cramped and narrow peristyle, dominated by moki steps framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. 

Virgil entered a wide and low liwan, tastefully offset by a fireplace. Virgil chose an exit at random and walked that way. 

Virgil entered a rough liwan, dominated by an obelisk framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, passing a reflection in a mirror. 

Virgil entered a peristyle, dominated by a fountain framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil walked away from that place. 

Virgil entered a high tetrasoon, tastefully offset by divans lining the perimeter. Virgil discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. 

Virgil entered a luxurious fogou, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a kiva, dominated by a moasic with a design of acanthus. Virgil thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Virgil entered a peristyle, dominated by a fireplace framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Virgil entered a high liwan, dominated by a fallen column framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil chose an exit at random and walked that way, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Virgil entered a high liwan, dominated by a fallen column framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a high liwan, dominated by a fallen column framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a high kiva, dominated by a trompe-l'oeil fresco with a design of acanthus. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. Quite unexpectedly Virgil reached the end of the labyrinth.

"And that was how it happened," Little Nemo said, ending his story.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Scheherazade told a very exciting story. Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Scheherazade told a very exciting story. Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a recursive house of many doors from which few emerged. Virgil couldn't quite say how he was wandering there. Virgil thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Virgil entered a luxurious tetrasoon, dominated by an obelisk with a design of arabseque. Virgil discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Virgil entered a cramped and narrow liwan, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Virgil chose an exit at random and walked that way, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Virgil entered a rough kiva, tastefully offset by a fountain. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a high kiva, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a liwan, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a cramped and narrow peristyle, tastefully offset by a moasic. Virgil discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, humming a little to relieve the silence. Which was where Virgil discovered the way out.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Scheherazade told a very exciting story. Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once an architectural forest that lived in eternal twilight. Marco Polo was almost certain about why he happened to be there. Marco Polo thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Marco Polo entered a luxurious fogou, tastefully offset by a koi pond. Marco Polo wandered, lost in thought, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Marco Polo entered a luxurious peristyle, dominated by a koi pond framed by a pattern of arabseque. Marco Polo thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Marco Polo chose an exit at random and walked that way. 

Marco Polo entered a luxurious fogou, tastefully offset by a trompe-l'oeil fresco. Marco Polo wandered, lost in thought. 

Marco Polo entered a tetrasoon, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Marco Polo opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Marco Polo muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Marco Polo opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Marco Polo felt a bit dizzy at the confusion of doors. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low tetrasoon, dominated by moki steps with a design of acanthus. Marco Polo muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. 

Marco Polo entered a fogou, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of arabseque. Marco Polo wandered, lost in thought. 

Marco Polo entered a luxurious tetrasoon, tastefully offset by divans lining the perimeter. Marco Polo muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. Which was where Marco Polo discovered the way out.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

Once upon a time, there was a blind poet named Homer, an explorer of Venice named Marco Polo and a poet exiled from Florence named Dante Alighieri. Homer suggested that he should tell a story, because it was Alex's birthday. So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Homer told a very exciting story. "And that was how it happened," Homer said, ending his story.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a twilight dimention in space from which few emerged. Virgil was almost certain about why he happened to be there. Virgil muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. 

Virgil entered a luxurious fogou, dominated by a sipapu which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Virgil wandered, lost in thought. 

Virgil entered a high liwan, dominated by an obelisk framed by a pattern of acanthus. Virgil muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. Which was where Virgil discovered the way out.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a vast and perilous maze with many forking paths. Homer had followed a secret path, and so he had arrived in that place. Homer discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, passing a reflection in a mirror. 

Homer entered a rough kiva, tastefully offset by a trompe-l'oeil fresco. Homer thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Homer entered a liwan, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Homer thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Homer entered a terrace, dominated by a fountain which was lined with a repeated pattern of arabseque. Homer felt a bit dizzy at the confusion of doors, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Homer entered a wide and low tetrasoon, dominated by a fountain with a design of arabseque. Homer felt a bit dizzy at the confusion of doors, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Homer entered a luxurious tetrasoon, dominated by a trompe-l'oeil fresco with a design of acanthus. Homer felt a bit dizzy at the confusion of doors. 

Homer entered a rough fogou, dominated by a fireplace which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Homer discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. At the darkest hour Homer found the exit.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." Then Scheherazade opened her book and read them the following page:

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a vast and perilous maze just on the other side of the garden wall. Murasaki Shikibu was almost certain about why she happened to be there. Murasaki Shikibu felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a luxurious kiva, dominated by a trompe-l'oeil fresco with a design of arabseque. Murasaki Shikibu wandered, lost in thought. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a high terrace, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Murasaki Shikibu opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a terrace, tastefully offset by a trompe-l'oeil fresco. Murasaki Shikibu discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a cramped and narrow liwan, dominated by a trompe-l'oeil fresco framed by a pattern of acanthus. Murasaki Shikibu thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a luxurious fogou, dominated by a sipapu which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Murasaki Shikibu muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened. 

Murasaki Shikibu entered a luxurious liwan, dominated by a koi pond framed by a pattern of arabseque. Murasaki Shikibu chose an exit at random and walked that way, not knowing that it was indeed the wrong way. Quite unexpectedly Murasaki Shikibu reached the end of the labyrinth.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." Then Scheherazade opened her book and read them the following page:

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a vast and perilous maze with many forking paths. Dunyazad didn't know why she happened to be there. Dunyazad felt sure that this must be the way out, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Dunyazad entered a rough kiva, tastefully offset by a fountain. Dunyazad felt sure that this must be the way out, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. 

Dunyazad entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Dunyazad felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Dunyazad entered a rough kiva, dominated by a fallen column with a design of arabseque. Dunyazad felt sure that this must be the way out, sparing a passing glance at a mirror. Almost unable to believe it, Dunyazad found the exit.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once a library with many forking paths. Marco Polo couldn't quite say how he was wandering there. Marco Polo walked away from that place. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Marco Polo thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Marco Polo entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Marco Polo chose an exit at random and walked that way. 

Marco Polo entered a kiva, tastefully offset by a fireplace. Marco Polo chose an exit at random and walked that way, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Marco Polo entered a high terrace, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Marco Polo walked away from that place, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Marco Polo entered a cramped and narrow peristyle, tastefully offset by a moasic. Marco Polo opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead. 

Marco Polo entered a high kiva, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Marco Polo opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Marco Polo entered a luxurious tetrasoon, dominated by a fireplace with a design of arabseque. Marco Polo thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. Quite unexpectedly Marco Polo found the exit.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

There was once an engmatic labyrinth with many forking paths. Dante Alighieri was lost, like so many before and after, and he had come to that place, as we all eventually must. Dante Alighieri wandered, lost in thought. 

Dante Alighieri entered a tetrasoon, dominated by a sipapu with a design of arabseque. Dante Alighieri muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Dante Alighieri entered a peristyle, dominated by a moasic framed by a pattern of arabseque. Dante Alighieri felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Dante Alighieri entered a wide and low liwan, tastefully offset by a fireplace. Dante Alighieri walked away from that place, not knowing that it was indeed the wrong way. 

Dante Alighieri entered a liwan, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Dante Alighieri thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way. 

Dante Alighieri entered a liwan, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Dante Alighieri walked away from that place. 

Dante Alighieri entered a wide and low liwan, tastefully offset by a moasic. Dante Alighieri thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Dante Alighieri entered a luxurious fogou, dominated by a sipapu which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Dante Alighieri walked away from that place. 

Dante Alighieri entered a high tetrasoon, tastefully offset by divans lining the perimeter. Dante Alighieri discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. 

Dante Alighieri entered a rough tetrasoon, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Dante Alighieri chose an exit at random and walked that way. 

Dante Alighieri entered a kiva, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Dante Alighieri discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Dante Alighieri entered a kiva, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Dante Alighieri felt a bit dizzy at the confusion of doors. 

Dante Alighieri entered a wide and low terrace, tastefully offset by a sipapu. Dante Alighieri discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Dante Alighieri entered a rough kiva, tastefully offset by a fountain. Dante Alighieri thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. Almost unable to believe it, Dante Alighieri discovered the way out.

Then Scheherazade ended her story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So she began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Scheherazade told:

Once upon a time, there was an English poet named Geoffery Chaucer, a Khagan of the Ikh Mongol Uls named Kublai Khan and a lady of the Imperial Court named Murasaki Shikibu. Geoffery Chaucer suggested that she should tell a story, because it was Alex's birthday. So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Geoffery Chaucer told:

Once upon a time, there was a philosopher named Socrates, a poet of Rome named Virgil and an English poet named Geoffery Chaucer. Geoffery Chaucer suggested that he should tell a story, because it was Alex's birthday. So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Geoffery Chaucer told:

Once upon a time, there was a poet exiled from Florence named Dante Alighieri, a blind poet named Homer and a poet of Rome named Virgil. Virgil suggested that he should tell a story, because it was Alex's birthday. So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." 

This is the story that Virgil told:

There was once a vast and perilous maze that had never known the light of the sun. Shahryar was lost, like so many before and after, and he had come to that place, as we all eventually must. Shahryar muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened, not knowing that it was indeed the wrong way. 

Shahryar entered a fogou, dominated by a moasic which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Shahryar felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Shahryar entered a kiva, tastefully offset by an obelisk. Shahryar chose an exit at random and walked that way. 

Shahryar entered a terrace, tastefully offset by a fireplace. Shahryar felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Shahryar entered a wide and low liwan, dominated by an obelisk framed by a pattern of acanthus. Shahryar thought that this direction looked promising, and went that way, humming a little to relieve the silence. 

Shahryar entered a high fogou, dominated by moki steps which was lined with a repeated pattern of acanthus. Shahryar felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Shahryar entered a high terrace, dominated by a koi pond which was lined with a repeated pattern of arabseque. Shahryar felt sure that this must be the way out. 

Shahryar entered a peristyle, tastefully offset by a fallen column. Shahryar opened a door, not feeling quite sure where it lead, passing a reflection in a mirror. 

Shahryar entered a wide and low liwan, dominated by an obelisk framed by a pattern of acanthus. Shahryar discovered that one of the doors lead somewhere else. 

Shahryar entered a rough kiva, tastefully offset by a fountain. Shahryar muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened, listening to the echo of footsteps. 

Shahryar entered a rough kiva, dominated by a moasic with a design of arabseque. Shahryar muttered, "North, this way is probably north!" as the door opened, listening to the echo of footsteps. Almost unable to believe it, Shahryar reached the end of the labyrinth.

Then Virgil ended his story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Virgil told a very exciting story. "And that was how it happened," Virgil said, ending his story.

Then Geoffery Chaucer ended his story, saying, "But there is another tale which is more marvelous still."

So he began, "It is related, O august king, that..." And Geoffery Chaucer told a very exciting story. "And that was how it happened," Geoffery Chaucer said, ending his story.
